وَلَوْ وَجَدَ فِي دَارِهِ مَعْدِنَا فَلَيْسَ فِيهِ شَيٍّ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا فِيهِ الْحُمُسُ لِلإطْلَاقِ مَا رَوَيْنَا وَلَهُ أَنَّهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْأَرْضِ مُرَكَّبٌ فِيهَا وَلَا مُؤْنَةَ فِى سَائِرِ الاجْزَاءِ فَكَذَا فِى هَذَا الْجُزْءِ لَاَنَّ الْجُزء لا يُخَالِفُ الْجُمْلَةَ بِخِلَافِ الكنز لأنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُرَّكَبِ فِيهَا
ترجمہ اور اگر اپنے گھر میں معدن پائی تو اس میں امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک کوئی چیز واجب نہ ہوگی۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا ہے کہ اس میں بھی خمس واجب ہے اس حدیث کے مطلق ہونے کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے روایت کی ہے اور امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ معدن زمین کے اجزاء سے زمین کے اندر مرکب ہے اور زمین کے اجزاء میں کوئی مونت اور بار نہیں ہے لہذا اس جز کے اندر بھی کوئی بار نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ جز اپنے کل سے مخالف نہیں ہوتا۔ برخلاف کنز ( دفینہ ) کے کیونکہ وہ زمین میں مرکب نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی انسان کو اپنے گھر کے اندر سے کوئی کان دستیاب ہو گئی تو اس کان میں امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک کوئی خمس واجب نہ ہوگا۔ صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ اس میں بھی خمس واجب ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ حدیث ” وَ فِي الرِّكَازِ الْحُمُسُ ، مطلق ہے اس میں زمین اور گھر کی کوئی تفصیل نہیں ہے اس لئے مطلقا معدن میں خمس واجب ہوگا خواہ گھر کے اندر دستیاب ہوئی ہو یا زمین کے اندر دستیاب ہوئی ہو۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ معدن خلقتہ گھر کی زمین کا ایک جز ہے اور گھر کے اجزاء میں سے کسی جز کے اندر خراج یا عشر وغیرہ امام وقت کے لئے کوئی مونت اور خرچہ واجب نہیں ہوتا لہذا اس معدنی جز کے اندر بھی کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ جز اپنے کل کے مخالف نہیں ہوتا۔ لہذا اس معدن جز کے اندر بھی کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ جز اپنے کل کے مخالف نہیں ہوتا اس کے برخلاف دفینہ کہ وہ زمین میں مرکب نہیں ہے یعنی زمین کا جز نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ وہ تو زمین کے اندر ودیعت رکھا ہوا ہے اس لئے اگر گھر کے اندر دفینہ مل جائے تو اس میں خمس واجب ہوگا سوال ... یہاں ایک سوال ہے وہ یہ کہ معدن اگر زمین کے اجزاء میں سے ہے تو اس کے ساتھ تیم کرنا جائز ہونا چاہئے حالانکہ بالا جماع معدن یعنی سونے یا چاندی وغیرہ سے تیتم کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ جواب تمیم کی اجازت اس چیز کے ساتھ ہے جو زمین کے جنس سے ہے نہ کہ زمین کے خلقی اور پیدائشی اجزاء کے ساتھ اور معدن زمین کی جنس سے نہیں ہے بلکہ زمین کے اجزاء میں سے ہے۔ بایں طور کہ زمین کی بیچ میں سونے چاندی کی کان بھی داخل ہو جاتی ہے۔
If he finds a mine in his house, then according to Abu Hanifa there is nothing (obligatory) in it, and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said that in it khums is obligatory due to the absoluteness of what we narrated. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that it is from the parts of the earth, composed in it, and there is no burden (mu'nah) in the rest of the parts, so likewise in this part, because the part does not differ from the whole, unlike a treasure (kanz) because it is not composed in it. Translation: And if he finds a mine in his house, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa no thing is wajib in it. And the two companions said that khums is wajib in it due to the absoluteness of the hadith that we narrated. And the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the mine is from the parts of the earth, composed in the earth, and in the parts of the earth there is no burden and levy, so in this part also no levy will be. Because the part does not oppose the whole. Unlike a treasure (buried thing) because it is not composed in the earth. Explanation: The issue is that if a person obtains a mine inside his house, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa no khums is wajib in it. The two companions said that khums is wajib in it. The proof of the two companions is that the hadith 'and in rikaz is khums' is absolute; there is no detail between land and house, so absolutely in a mine khums is wajib whether it is found in a house or in land. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the mine is a part of the land of the house by creation, and among the parts of the house, no kharaj or 'ushr etc. is wajib for the imam, so in this mineral part also nothing is wajib. Because the part does not oppose the whole. Therefore, in this mineral part also nothing is wajib. Because the part does not oppose the whole, unlike a treasure because it is not composed in the earth, meaning it is not a part of the earth, but rather it is deposited inside the earth. So if a treasure is found in a house, khums is wajib. Question: There is a question here: If the mine is from the parts of the earth, then tayammum with it should be permissible, whereas by consensus tayammum with a mine, i.e., gold or silver etc., is not permissible. Answer: Permission for tayammum is with something that is of the same genus as earth, not with the created and innate parts of the earth, and the mine is not of the genus of earth, but rather it is from the parts of the earth. In such a way that a gold or silver mine is included within the earth.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of finding a mine inside one's own house. It presents the intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no khums is due because the mine is a part of the land, and the land already bears no tax (mu'nah) on its parts, so this part should similarly be free. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the general hadith on rikaz applies to all mines, regardless of location, so khums is wajib. The author explains the reasoning on each side, including the distinction between a mine (which is composed in the earth) and a buried treasure (kanz) which is not. A subsidiary question about tayammum with minerals is answered, clarifying that tayammum is permitted only with something of the same genus as earth (i.e., pure earth), not with minerals like gold or silver, even though they are parts of the earth by composition.

Key Terms
دَار (dar): House, residential property including its land.
جُزْء (juz'): Part, component of the earth.
كَنْز (kanz): Buried treasure, not composed in the earth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is khums not due on a mine found in one's house?
A) Because the hadith on rikaz is not authentic.
B) Because the mine is a part of the earth, and parts have no tax burden.
C) Because the mine is considered a hidden treasure.
D) Because the owner is the state.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a mine found in one's own house is subject to an intra-school dispute: Abu Hanifa exempts it based on the principle that parts follow the whole, while his two companions impose khums based on the general hadith on rikaz.

Mine in Owned Land: Two Narrations from Abu Hanifa
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khums for mine in owned land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two narrations from Abu Hanifa regarding khums on a mine in owned land and identify the basis of distinction used by each narration.

Core Idea
A mine in owned land: Abu Hanifa has two narrations; the distinction hinges on whether land carries tax burdens (mu'nah) unlike a house.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the obligation of khums on a mine discovered in one's own owned land. Imam Abu Hanifa has two narrations — one from al-Mabsut that no khums is due, and another from al-Jami' al-Saghir that khums is wajib. The two companions (Sahibayn) uniformly hold that khums is wajib. We will analyze the reasoning behind each view and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage deals with a mine found in owned land (not a house). Recognize that the default ruling for a mine in a house is no khums.
2
State the two narrations from Abu Hanifa
From al-Mabsut: no khums; from al-Jami' al-Saghir: khums wajib. Note that Sahibayn hold khums wajib.
3
Analyze the basis of distinction
The Jami' Saghir narration differentiates land from house because land carries 'ushr and kharaj (mu'nah), while a house does not. Therefore, similarly, khums applies to mines in land.
This is the wajh al-farq (ground of distinction).
4
Determine the practical consequence
According to the Mabsut narration, the finder keeps all; according to Jami' Saghir and Sahibayn, he pays khums. The mufta bihi is not specified.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether payment is required.

قَالَ وَإِنْ وَجَدَ فِي أَرْضِهِ فَعَنْ أَبِي حَنِيْفَةَ فِيْهِ رِوَايَتَانِ وَوَجُهُ الْفَرْقِ عَلَى إِحْدَاهُمَا وَهُوَ رِوَايَةُ الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ أنَّ الدارَ مَلَكَتْ خَالِيَةٌ عَنِ الْمَونِ دُونَ الْأرْضِ وَلِهَذَا وَجَبَ الْعُشْرُ وَالْخَرَاجُ فِي الْأَرْضِ دُونَ الدَّارِ فَكَذَا هذِهِ الْمَوْنَة
ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے اپنی مملوکہ زمین میں معدن کو پایا تو امام ابو حنیفہ سے اس میں دو روایتیں ہیں اور وجہ فرق ان دونوں میں سے ایک روایت پر ، اور وہ روایت جامع صغیر کی ہے یہ ہے کہ گھر اس حال میں مملوک ہوا کہ وہ تمام مشقتوں اور خرچہ سے خالی ہے ( اور ) زمین ایسی نہیں ہے اسی وجہ سے عشر اور خراج زمین میں واجب ہے نہ کہ گھر میں پس یو نہی یہ خرچہ بھی۔ تشریح اگر کسی نے اپنی مملوکہ زمین میں کان برآمد کی تو اس بارے میں حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ سے دور وایتیں ہیں ۔ مبسوط کی روایت تو یہ ہے کہ اس میں خمس وغیرہ کوئی چیز واجب نہ ہوگی جیسا کہ گھر کے اندر برآمد ہونے والی کان میں کچھ واجب نہیں ہوتا اور جامع صغیر کی روایت یہ ہے کہ اس میں خمس واجب ہوگا۔ صاحبین کی ایک ہی روایت ہے کہ اس میں خمس واجب ہوگا ۔ اور صاحبین کا مستدل حدیث ” وَ فِى الرِّكَازِ الْخُمُسَ “ کا مطلق ہونا ہے اور مبسوط کی روایت کے مطابق امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مملوکہ زمین کے کسی جز میں خمس واجب نہیں ہے لہذا اس معدنی جز میں بھی خمس واجب نہ ہوگا اور جامع صغیر کی روایت کے مطابق مملوکہ زمین اور گھر کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ زمین مونتوں اور مشقتوں سے خالی نہیں ہوتی اور گھر ان چیزوں سے خالی ہوتا ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ زمین میں عشر اور خراج واجب ہوتا ہے مگر گھر کے اندر یہ چیزیں واجب نہیں ہوتیں پس اسی طرح خمس کی مونت زمین میں نکلنے والی کان کے اندر واجب ہوگی اور گھر کے اندر نکلنے والی کان میں واجب نہ ہوگی۔
He said: And if he finds a mine in his land, then from Abu Hanifa there are two narrations regarding it. And the basis of distinction according to one of them, which is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, is that the house was owned free from burden (mu'nah) unlike the land, and therefore 'ushr and kharaj are wajib on the land and not on the house, so likewise this burden (khums). Translation: And if he finds a mine in his owned land, then from Imam Abu Hanifa there are two narrations regarding it. And the basis of distinction on one of those narrations, which is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, is that the house was owned in a state free from all hardships and expenses, but the land is not so. For this reason, 'ushr and kharaj are wajib on the land, not on the house, so too this expense. Explanation: If someone discovers a mine in his owned land, then regarding this there are two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. The narration of al-Mabsut is that in it khums etc. no thing is wajib, just as in a mine discovered inside a house nothing is wajib. And the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir is that in it khums is wajib. There is a single narration from the two companions that in it khums is wajib. And the proof of the two companions is the absoluteness of the hadith 'and in rikaz is khums'. And according to the narration of al-Mabsut, the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that in any part of owned land, khums is not wajib, so in this mineral part also khums is not wajib. And according to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, the basis of distinction between owned land and house is that land is not free from burdens and hardships, while a house is free from these things. For this reason, 'ushr and kharaj are wajib on the land but not inside the house. So similarly, the burden of khums will be wajib on a mine found in land and not on a mine found in a house.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling for a mine found in one's own owned land. It presents two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa: one from al-Mabsut that nothing is due (analogous to the house case), and another from al-Jami' al-Saghir that khums is wajib. The two companions (Sahibayn) consistently hold khums wajib. The distinction between house and land is based on the fact that land is subject to 'ushr and kharaj (agricultural taxes), whereas a house is free from such burdens. Similarly, the burden of khums applies to mines in land but not in houses according to the second narration. The mufta bihi position is not explicitly stated, but the text presents all views.

Key Terms
أَرْض (ard): Owned land (as opposed to a house).
رِوَايَة (riwayah): Narration, transmission of a legal opinion from Imam Abu Hanifa.
مُؤْنَة (mu'nah): Burden, tax, expense (such as 'ushr and kharaj).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, why is khums wajib on a mine found in owned land but not in a house?
A) Because land is owned by the state
B) Because land is subject to 'ushr and kharaj, while a house is free from such burdens
C) Because a house is not considered property
D) Because the hadith specifies land

Key Takeaway
The disposition of khums on a mine in owned land is disputed within the Hanafi school, with the distinction centered on whether the land carries tax burdens (mu'nah).

Ruling on Rikaz: Khums Obligation and Sub-Cases
Establishes ruling on rikaz, its categories, and distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rikaz, apply the categorization by markings to determine khums or luqata, and evaluate the distribution dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Rikaz: khums is wajib; four-fifths go to finder (mubah land) or allottee (owned land per majority view).

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on rikaz (buried treasure) as presented in al-Hidayah. The text establishes the obligation of khums based on a sound hadith, then categorizes treasure by its markings—Islamic markings make it luqata, while Jahili markings always require khums. The most nuanced part is the distribution: whether the finder or the original landowner gets the remaining four-fifths. A well-known intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf hinges on the nature of possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify what rikaz is (buried treasure, not minerals) and the obligation: khums (one-fifth) is wajib based on the hadith.
The term rikaz derives from rukz (planting), indicating it is man-made.
2
Categorize by markings
Determine the category based on the treasure's markings: Islamic markings (e.g., shahadah) → treated as luqata; Jahili markings (e.g., idol) → khums always wajib; ambiguous → default Jahili but later opinion may treat as Islamic.
For Islamic markings, the finder must announce it like lost property, not pay khums immediately.
3
Determine the land type
Identify whether the treasure was found in mubah (unowned) land or owned (conquered and distributed) land. This affects who gets the four-fifths after khums.
Mubah land: no prior owner; owned land: potentially an original allottee.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
For owned land, Abu Yusuf holds the finder gets four-fifths (complete possession), while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it goes to the original allottee (special ownership extends to contents).
The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is relied upon in fatwa.
5
Apply the analogy
The text uses the analogy of a fish with a pearl: selling the fish does not transfer the pearl, just as selling the land does not transfer the treasure buried inside, because it is deposited, not part of the land.
This distinguishes from minerals (ma'din) which are part of the land and thus transfer with it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِن جَدَ رِكَازا أى كَنُزَا وَجَبَ فِيهِ الْحُمْسُ عِنْدَهُمْ لِمَارَوَيْنَا وَاسْمُ الرِّكَازِ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الْكَبْرِ لِمَعنى الرُّكَّز وَهُوَ الْإِنْبَات ثُمَّ إِنْ كَانَ عَلى ضَرْب أهلِ الإِسْلامِ كَالْمُكْتُوبِ عَلَيْهِ كَلِمَةِ الشَّهَادَةِ فَهُوَ بِمَزَلَةِ النُّقْطَةِ وَقَدْ عُرِفَ حُكْمَهَا فِى مَوْضِعِهَا وَإِنْ كَانَ عَلَى ضَرَبِ أهْلِ الْجَاهِلِيَّةِ كَالْمُنقُوشِ عَلَيْهِ الصَّنَم فَفِيهِ الخُمْسُ عَلى كُلِّ حَالٍ لِمَانِيَّنَا ثُمَّ إِنْ وَجَدَهُ فِي أَرْضِ مُبَاحَةٍ فَأَرْبَعَةُ أَحْمَاسِم لِلْوَاجِدِ لِأَنَّهُ تَمَّ الأَحْرَازُ مِنْهُ إِذ لَا عِلْمَ بِهِ لِلْغَانِمِينَ فَيَخْتَصُّ هُوَ بِهِ وَإِنْ وَجَدَهُ فِي أَرْضِ مَمْلُو كَةٍ فَكَذَا الحِكْمُ عِندَ أَبِى يُوسُفَ لِأَنَّ الْإِسْتِحْفَاقَ بِتَمَامِ الْحَيَازَةِ وَ هُوَ مِنه وَعِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ هُوَ لِلْمُحْتَطَ لَهُ وَ هُوَ الَّذِئ مَلَكَهُ الإِمَامُ هَذِهِ الْبَقَعَةَ أَوَّلَ الْفَتْحِ لأَنَّهُ سَقَتَ يَدُهُ إِلَيْهِ وَ هِيَ يَدُ الخُصُوصِ فَيَمْلِكُ بِهِ مَا فِى الْبَاطِنِ وإِنْ كَانَتْ عَلَى الظَّاهِرِ كَمَنْ اِصْطَادَ سَمَكَةً فِي بَطْنِهَا دُرَةً ثُمَّ بِالْبَيْعِ لَمْ يَخْرُجْ عَنْ مِلَكِم لأنهُ مُودَعْ فِيهَا بِخِلَافِ الْمَعْدِنِ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ أَجْزَائِهَا فَيَنْتَقِلُ إِلَى الْمُشْتَرِى وَإِن لَم يَعْرِفِ الْمُخْتَطَ لَمْ يَصْرِفُ إلى أقصى مَالِكَ يُعْرَفُ فِي الْإِسْلَامِ عَلى مَا قَالُوا وَلَوِ اشْتَبَهَ الضّرب يَـ يجعل جَاهِلِيَّا فِى ظَاهِرِ الْمَذْهَبِ لِأَنَّهُ الأَصل وَقِيلَ يُجْعَل إِسْلَامِيَّا فِي زَمَانِنَا لِتَقَادُمِ الْعَهْدِ
And if he finds rikaz, meaning treasure, then khums (one-fifth) is obligatory upon it according to them (the Hanafis) due to what we have narrated. The term rikaz is applied to [buried] treasure due to the meaning of rukz, which is planting. Then if it is upon the stamp of the people of Islam, like having the word of shahadah written on it, then it is in the position of luqata (lost property), and its ruling is known in its place. And if it is upon the stamp of the people of Jahiliyyah, like having an idol engraved on it, then khums is obligatory upon it in all cases according to our proof. Then if he finds it in mubah (unowned) land, four-fifths are for the finder, because complete acquisition from him is achieved, since the ghani (conquerors) have no knowledge of it, so he is specifically entitled to it. And if he finds it in owned land, the same ruling applies according to Abu Yusuf, because entitlement is based on complete possession, and he (the finder) has it. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it belongs to the mukhtat lahu (the one to whom the land was allotted), and he is the one to whom the Imam granted ownership of this plot at the time of the initial conquest, because his hand reached it, and it is a hand of specific ownership, so by it he owns what is inside (the land), even if (his ownership) is only on the apparent (surface). Like one who catches a fish with a pearl in its stomach, then by selling (the fish), the pearl does not go out of his ownership because it is deposited inside it, unlike a mine because it is part of the mine (the land), so it transfers to the buyer. And if the mukhtat lahu is not known, it is turned over to the last known owner in Islam, as they said. And if the stamp is ambiguous, then in the apparent madhhab it is made Jahili because it is the original, and it was said that in our time it should be made Islamic due to the length of time.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on rikaz (buried treasure) from Kitab al-Zakat of al-Hidayah. The commentator first establishes the basic ruling: finding rikaz obligates khums (one-fifth) by consensus of the Hanafi school, based on the hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums'. The term rikaz is distinguished from ma'din (minerals) by its origin—rikaz is man-made treasure buried in the ground, while minerals are naturally created. The passage then divides rikaz into three categories based on markings: (1) If it bears Islamic markings (e.g., Shahadah), it is treated as luqata (lost property) and not subject to khums; instead, the finder must announce it for a period. (2) If it bears Jahili markings (e.g., idol), khums is always wajib, whether the finder is adult/minor, free/slave, Muslim/dhimmi, and whether the treasure is gold, silver, brass, or other. (3) If markings are ambiguous, the apparent madhhab treats it as Jahili because that is the default, though some later scholars suggested making it Islamic due to the passage of time. The remainder after khums (four-fifths) is distributed based on the land: if found in mubah (unowned) land, it all goes to the finder because no one else had knowledge or possession. If found in owned land (i.e., conquered and distributed land), there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the finder (whether owner or not) gets the four-fifths because he physically possesses it. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that it goes to the original allottee (mukhtat lahu) because his special ownership extends to what is inside the land, even if he only possesses the surface. The passage gives an analogy: a fish with a pearl—selling the fish does not transfer the pearl because it is deposited inside, unlike minerals which are part of the land and thus transfer with it. If the allottee is unknown, the treasure goes to the last known owner in Islam.

Key Terms
رِكَاز (rikaz): Buried treasure from pre-Islamic or Islamic times, not including minerals.
الخُمْس (al-khums): One-fifth (20%) of the treasure, to be given to the state treasury for charitable purposes.
أرض مباحة (ard mubahah): Unowned land (e.g., wilderness) that has not been conquered or privately owned.
مختط له (mukhtat lahu): The original grantee of conquered land from the Imam.
لُقَطَة (luqata): Lost property that must be announced for a period before the finder can use it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person finds a treasure with Islamic engravings in mubah land, what is the initial ruling?
A) Khums is wajib immediately.
B) It is treated as luqata; the finder must announce it.
C) The finder keeps all of it.
D) It goes to the state treasury entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on rikaz hinges on markings (Islamic vs Jahili) and land type (mubah vs owned), with a settled preference for the allottee on owned land.

Khums on Pre-Islamic Treasure
Focus on the ruling for pre-Islamic treasure (kufr signs).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling of khums on pre-Islamic treasure and explain its basis in the hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
Pre-Islamic treasure is subject to khums (20%) as war booty, regardless of finder, material, or location.

This lesson focuses on treasure found with pre-Islamic signs (e.g., idol images). According to the Hanafi school, such treasure is treated as ghanimah (booty) and thus khums is wajib. We will examine the textual and rational proofs that establish this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Treasure Category
From the passage, recall the three categories of discovered treasure. Focus on the second: treasure with pre-Islamic signs (kufr).
Pre-Islamic signs include idol engravings or other non-Islamic symbols.
2
State the Ruling
The passage states that for pre-Islamic treasure, khums (one-fifth) is wajib in all cases, regardless of the finder's age, freedom, or religion, and regardless of the material or location.
This ruling is by consensus (bil ittifaq) of the Hanafis.
3
Examine the Textual Proof (Dalil Naqli)
The hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums' (On rikaz, the khums) is cited. In Hanafi usul, this ahad hadith establishes wujub.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalalah.
4
Examine the Rational Proof (Dalil Aqli)
The passage gives a qiyas: the land and treasure were in possession of disbelievers; upon Muslim conquest, it becomes ghanimah, on which khums is wajib.
This analogy strengthens the ruling.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Consider a finder who is a minor or a dhimmi; the khums is still due. Consider the material (brass, iron); it still applies.
The ruling is universal within this category.

كتاب الزكوة

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد سوم وروں کے درمیان مشترک ہے اور معدن کا بیان پہلے ہو چکا ہے تو اب کنز ہی مراد ہو گا رہی یہ بات کہ رکاز کا اطلاق معدن اور کنز دونوں میں کیوں ہوتا ہے؟ تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ رکاز رکز سے مشتق ہے اور کنز کے معنی ثابت کرنے کے ہیں اور معدن اور کنز دونوں زمین میں ثابت ہوتے ہیں بس فرق اتنا ہے کہ معدن از جانب خالق ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ اور کنز از جانب مخلوق ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر زمین میں کوئی دفینہ برآمد ہوا تو باتفاق احناف اس میں خمس ( پانچواں حصہ ) واجب ہوگا پھر اس دفینہ کی تین صورتیں ہیں یا تو اس پر علامات اسلام میں سے کوئی علامت ہو گی مثلا کلمہ شہادت لا الہ الا للہ لکھا ہوا ہے یا زمانہ جاہلیت کی کوئی علامت موجودگی مثلا اس پر کسی بت کی تصویر ہے یا اس کا حال مشتبہ ہو گا کہ اس کا اسلامی یا کفری ہو نا متعین اور مشخص نہیں ہوتا پس اگر پہلی صورت ہے یعنی اس دفینہ پر علامات اسلام موجود ہیں تو وہ لقطہ کے حکم میں ہے کیونکہ علامات اسلام کی وجہ سے یہ بات واضح ہوگئی ہے کہ یہ کسی مسلمان کا مال ہے اور مسلمان کے مال کو غنیمت نہیں بنایا جاتا لہذا اس میں خمس وغیرہ کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا بلکہ اس کا لقطہ قرار دیا جائے گا اور لقطہ کا حکم یہ ہے کہ ایک مدت تک اس کا اعلان کرایا جائے اب اگر اس مدت میں اس کا مالک معلوم ہو گیا تو وہ مال اس کو دیدیا جائے ورنہ یہ شخص اگر خود فقیر ہو تو اپنی ذات پر صدقہ کرے۔ اور اگر خود مالدار ہے تو کسی فقیر کو دیدے اور اس کو یہ بھی اختیار ہے کہ اس مال کو ہمیشہ اپنے پاس رکھے نہ خود خرچ کرے اور نہ فقیر کو دے اب رہی یہ بات کہ کتنی مدت اعلان کرائے تو اس کا حکم مال کی کمی اور زیادتی کی وجہ سے مختلف ہوتا رہتا ہے چنانچہ صاحب کفایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ دس درہم اور اس سے زائد میں ایک سال تک اعلان کرایا جائے اور دس درہم سے کم میں تین ماہ تک اور تین درہم سے کم میں ایک درہم تک ایک دن اعلان کرائے۔ اور ایک درہم سے کم میں دائیں بائیں دیکھ کر کسی فقیر کے ہاتھ میں رکھ دے۔

اور اگر وہ دفینہ ایسا ہے جس پر کفر کی علامات موجود ہیں تو اس میں ہر حال میں خمس واجب ہوگا۔ پانے والا شخص بالغ ہو یا نابالغ آزاد ہو یا غلام ہو۔ مسلمان ہو یا ذمی ہو۔ اور جو دفینہ برآمد ہوا ہے وہ سونا ہو یا چاندی ہو پیتل ہو یا لوہا یا اس کے علاوہ ہو یہ دفینہ اپنی مملوکہ زمین میں پایا ہو یا دوسرے کی زمین میں یا ایسی زمین میں پایا ہو جس کا کوئی مالک نہ ہو۔ دلیل نقلی اور عقلی اول باب میں گذر چکی ہے دلیل نقلی "وفی الركاز الخمس" ہے اور عقلی دلیل یہ ہے کہ زمین اور زمین کے اندر کا دفینہ سب کفار کے قبضہ میں تھا لیکن جب مسلمان غلبہ اس پر قابض ہو گئے تو یہ دفینہ مال غنیمت ہو گیا اور مال غنیمت میں خمس واجب ہوتا ہے لہذا اس میں بھی خمس واجب ہو گا۔
Book of Zakat. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume three. [The ruling of rikaz] is shared between [maden and kanz], and the discussion of maden has already passed, so now kanz is intended. As to why the term rikaz applies to both maden and kanz, the answer: rikaz is derived from rakaza, and kanz means something fixed; both maden and kanz are fixed in the earth. The only difference is that maden is fixed by the Creator, and kanz is fixed by creation. The substance of the issue: If a treasure (defīnah) is discovered in the earth, by consensus of the Hanafis, one-fifth (khums) is obligatory upon it. Then this treasure has three forms: either it has some sign of Islam on it, e.g. the kalimah 'la ilaha illallah' is written, or it has some sign of the Jahiliyyah period, e.g. an image of an idol, or its condition is ambiguous, i.e. whether it is Islamic or un-Islamic is uncertain and not specific. Therefore if it is the first case, i.e. the treasure has signs of Islam, then it falls under the ruling of luqtah (lost property), because due to the signs of Islam it has become clear that this belongs to a Muslim, and a Muslim's property cannot be taken as booty; hence no khums etc. is obligatory, rather it is declared luqtah. And the ruling of luqtah is that it should be announced for a period. Now if during this period its owner becomes known, the property is given to him; otherwise, if this person himself is poor, he may give it as sadaqah for himself, and if he is wealthy, he gives it to a poor person. He also has the option to keep the property with himself forever, neither spending it himself nor giving it to the poor. Now as to how long the announcement should be made: its ruling varies according to the scarcity or abundance of the property. Thus the author of Kifayah said: for ten dirhams or more, announce for one year; for less than ten dirhams, for three months; for less than three dirhams, for one dirham's worth, announce for one day; and for less than one dirham, look left and right and place it in the hand of a poor person.

And if the treasure has signs of disbelief, then in all cases khums is obligatory. Whether the finder is an adult or minor, free or slave, Muslim or dhimmi. And regardless of whether the treasure is gold, silver, brass, iron, or anything else, whether found on his own owned land, another's land, or land with no owner. The textual and rational proofs have passed in the first chapter: the textual proof is "and on rikaz the khums" (hadith), and the rational proof is that the land and the treasure within it were all in the possession of the disbelievers, but when the Muslims conquered and gained control, this treasure became war booty, and on war booty khums is obligatory; therefore khums is obligatory on this as well.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with the types of treasure (rikaz/defīnah) and their rulings. The author first clarifies that rikaz includes both mineral deposits (ma'dan) and buried treasure (kanz), but the discussion here focuses on buried treasure. The ruling is that any discovered treasure generally requires khums (one-fifth) by Hanafi consensus. However, the treasure falls into three categories: (1) with Islamic signs (e.g., the shahadah), (2) with pre-Islamic signs (e.g., idol images), and (3) ambiguous (mush tabih).

If Islamic signs are present, the treasure is treated as luqtah (lost property) because it clearly belonged to a Muslim and cannot be treated as booty. No khums is due. Instead, the finder must announce it for a specific period based on value: one year for 10+ dirhams, three months for 3-10 dirhams, one day for 1-3 dirhams, and immediate charity for less than one dirham. If the owner appears, it is returned; otherwise, the finder can keep it and give charity if poor, or if wealthy, must give to the poor.

If pre-Islamic signs are present, khums is obligatory regardless of the finder's status (age, freedom, religion) and regardless of the material or location of the find. The textual proof is the hadith "On rikaz (there is) the khums" (Fi al-rikaz al-khums), and the rational proof is that the land and treasure were originally in the possession of disbelievers, and upon Muslim conquest, it becomes ghanimah (booty), on which khums is wajib.

The ambiguous case (neither sign) is not elaborated here but will be discussed later. The passage establishes the foundational distinction between Islamic and non-Islamic treasure.

Key Terms
رکاز (rikaz): Buried treasure (kanz) or mineral deposits (ma'dan) found in the earth; derived from rakaza (to fix).
خمس (khums): One-fifth (20%) share that is obligatory on certain properties like rikaz and ghanimah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, on whom is khums obligatory for pre-Islamic treasure?
A) Only Muslim, adult, free finders
B) Only if the treasure is gold or silver
C) On the finder regardless of age, freedom, or religion
D) Only if found on one's own land

Key Takeaway
Pre-Islamic treasure is subject to khums as wajib, supported by the hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums' and the analogy to war booty.

Treasure with Islamic Signs: Ruling of Luqtah
Focus on the ruling for treasure with Islamic signs as luqtah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of luqtah to a treasure found with Islamic signs and determine the announcement period based on value.

Core Idea
Treasure with Islamic signs is luqtah (lost property) — no khums, but must be announced for a prescribed period.

When treasure bears Islamic symbols like the shahadah, it is considered the property of a Muslim, not booty. The Hanafi school treats it as luqtah, with specific announcement periods depending on value. This lesson details those periods and the finder's options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Treasure Category
From the passage, recall the three categories. Focus on the first: treasure with Islamic signs (e.g., shahadah, Quranic verses).
Islamic signs indicate Muslim ownership.
2
Understand the Ruling
Such treasure is not subject to khums; it is treated as luqtah (lost property). The finder must announce it.
No khums because it is not ghanimah; it belongs to a Muslim.
3
Learn the Announcement Periods
The passage, quoting Sahib Kifayah, gives specific periods based on value: 10+ dirhams: 1 year; 3-10 dirhams: 3 months; 1-3 dirhams: 1 day; less than 1 dirham: immediate charity.
These periods are to find the owner.
4
Know the Finder's Options
After announcement, if owner appears, return it. If not, the finder may keep it; if poor, he may use it; if wealthy, he must give to the poor.
The finder can also keep indefinitely without spending.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Consider a treasure worth 5 dirhams with the shahadah. Announce for 3 months.
Value determines the announcement duration.

Key Terms
لقطہ (luqtah): Lost property found by someone, which must be announced and eventually given to the owner or disposed of as charity.
علامات اسلام (alamaat al-Islam): Signs of Islam, such as the shahadah, Quranic verses, or Islamic symbols clearly indicating Muslim ownership.

Pre-Islamic Treasure in Ownerless vs Owned Land
Intra-Hanafi dispute on treasure in owned conquered land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pre-Islamic treasure found in ownerless and owned land, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn regarding entitlement to four-fifths after khums.

Core Idea
Who gets the treasure? Finder or original allocatee? It depends on whether possession is general or specific.

This passage from the Hidayah presents a detailed ruling on pre-Islamic (jahili) treasure. After stating the basic rule for ownerless land, it introduces a key intra-Hanafi dispute concerning owned land that was originally allocated by the Imam. The core of the dispute is whether the finder's actual possession or the prior specific constructive possession of the original allocatee (mukhatt lahu) gives entitlement to the four-fifths after khums. The author uses rational proofs and analogies to support both views, highlighting the importance of the concept of possession (qabdah) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between treasure found in ownerless land and that found in owned (mamluka) land. The ruling for ownerless land is unanimous among the Hanafis.
Ownerless land: no owner at all (e.g., mawat, conquered land before allocation).
2
State the ruling for ownerless land
One khums (1/5) is taken for the poor, and the remaining four-fifths belong to the finder. The proof is based on the combination of constructive possession (ghazis) and actual possession (finder).
This is the base case (asl) for the subsequent qiyas.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute for owned land
When the land is owned, specifically land originally allocated by the Imam (mukhatt lahu), Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the finder gets four-fifths (as in ownerless land), while the Sahibayn (Muhammad al-Shaybani and likely Zufar) hold that the original allocatee (mukhatt lahu) or his heirs get it.
The term 'tarafain' here refers to two of the three main disciples, probably Muhammad and Zufar, since Abu Yusuf is mentioned seperately.
4
Analyze the proofs and the objection
Imam Abu Yusuf argues that entitlement comes from complete preservation (tamam-e-hifazat) by the finder. The Sahibayn argue that the prior constructive possession of the mukhatt lahu is specific (khususi) and thus effective, making him the owner of hidden property. An objection is raised that constructive possession does not confer ownership, but the answer distinguishes between general and specific constructive possession.
The fish-and-pearl analogy is used to illustrate specific constructive possession.
5
Examine the furu' (branch ruling) on sale of land
If the mukhatt lahu sells the land and later a treasure is found, the four-fifths still belong to the seller (mukhatt lahu) because the treasure is considered a deposit (wadi'ah) inside the land, not part of it. This is unlike minerals (ma'dan), which transfer with the land.
This furu' strengthens the Sahibayn's position by showing continuity of ownership.

ثُمَّ ثم بالبيع الخ سے یہ فرمایا کہ مختط لہ نے اگر زمین فروخت کردی پھر اس زمین میں سے کوئی دفینہ بر آمد ہوا تو بھی چار خمس مختط لہ کے ہوں گے کیونکہ دفینہ زمین کے اندر ودیعت رکھا ہوا ہے اس لئے زمین فروخت کرنے سے وہ دفینہ اس کے مالک سے خارج نہ ہوگا جیسے کسی نے شکار کرنے کے بعد مچھلی فروخت کی اور مچھلی کے پیٹ سے موتی نکلا تو یہ موتی فروخت کر دینے سے اس کی ملک سے خارج نہیں ہوگا بر خلاف معدن کے کہ وہ زمین کی بیع سے مشتری کی طرف منتقل ہو جائے گی کیونکہ معدن ( کان ) زمین کے اجزاء میں سے ہے پس جب زمین مشتری کی طرف منتقل ہو گی تو اس کے تمام اجزاء بھی مشتری کی طرف منتقل ہو جائیں گے۔
The author of Hidayah says: The treasure on which there are signs of disbelief, if it is discovered from land which has no owner, then one-fifth is taken out and the remaining four-fifths belong to the finder. Because the complete possession and gathering of this treasure was done by this finder. And the ghazis had no knowledge of this treasure, so this person is specifically entitled to it. And when he is specifically entitled, then the four-fifths are also his. The essence of this proof is what has passed earlier: that the ghazis have constructive (hukman) possession over this treasure, and the finder has actual (haqiqatan) possession. Therefore, considering the constructive possession, one khums is taken for the orphans and the poor, and considering the actual possession, four khums are given to the finder. And if this pre-Islamic treasure is found on some owned (mamluka) land, then according to Imam Abu Yusuf the same ruling applies: one khums is given to the poor, and the remaining four khums belong to the finder, whether the finder is the owner of the land or not. Because entitlement (istiḥqāq) arises from complete preservation (tamām-e-ḥifāẓat), and complete preservation was done by this finder; therefore he is the one entitled to the four khums. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, i.e. the Sahibayn) have said: one khums is taken out and the remaining four khums belong to the mukhatt lahu (the one to whom the land was allocated by the Imam at the time of conquest, with a line drawn to mark its boundaries). So the person entitled to four khums is the mukhatt lahu. And if he is not present, then his heirs are entitled, even if several generations down, because after the conquest the first possession was that of the mukhatt lahu. There is an objection to the proof of the two: that the possession of the mukhatt lahu over the treasure, although prior, is constructive (hukman), because actual possession belongs to the finder, and constructive possession does not make one the owner of the treasure, as has passed regarding the ghazis: they are not the owners. The answer: Constructive possession certainly does not prove ownership, but this is when the constructive possession is general (ala sabil al-umum), as the ghazis' possession is general, all ghazis being owners in a general sense. However, if constructive possession is specific (khususi), i.e. a single person is specifically the owner, then due to this constructive possession he will also become the owner of things inside the earth, even if his actual possession is only on the apparent surface. For example, a person catches a fish, and a pearl is found in its stomach; the owner of this pearl is the same person who owns the fish.

Then (thumma) from 'thumma bil-bay' etc., it is said: If the mukhatt lahu sold the land, and then a treasure is discovered from that land, the four khums still belong to the mukhatt lahu, because the treasure is deposited inside the land, so selling the land does not remove it from his ownership. Just as if someone sold a fish after catching it, and a pearl is found in its stomach, the pearl is not removed from his ownership by the sale. This is unlike minerals (ma'dan), which transfer to the buyer upon sale of land, because minerals are part of the land's components; so when the land transfers to the buyer, all its components also transfer to the buyer.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on pre-Islamic treasure (jaahili defīnah) and includes an intra-madhhab dispute. The author of Hidayah states that if the treasure is found in ownerless (mawāt) land, one-fifth (khums) is taken and distributed to the poor/orphans, and the remaining four-fifths go to the finder. This is because the ghazis (conquerors) have constructive possession (hukman) over the land, entitling them to the khums, while the finder has actual possession (haqiqatan), making him entitled to the rest.

If the treasure is found in owned land (mamluka), there is a difference of opinion. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the finder, not the landowner, gets the four-fifths (after khums), because entitlement comes from the act of preservation (ihraz), which the finder performed. The Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa's two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani? But here 'tarafain' likely refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, though Abu Yusuf is mentioned separately; the text says 'Hazrat Imam Abu Yusuf ke nazdik... aur tarafain ne farmaya...' so 'tarafain' here is probably Muhammad al-Shaybani and someone else, possibly Zufar? Actually in Hidayah, 'tarafain' often refers to the two Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. But here Abu Yusuf's view is stated separately, so 'tarafain' might refer to Muhammad and Zufar or possibly the later scholars. Most likely, the two are Muhammad and Abu Yusuf? However the text says 'Imam Abu Yusuf ke nazdik' and then 'aur tarafain ne farmaya' so it is an ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf alone and the other two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad? But usually 'tarafain' means two of the three main Hanafi imams, often Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Here it seems Abu Yusuf's view is given separately, so 'tarafain' likely means Muhammad and Zufar? Or it could be a copy error. I'll note this in the commentary.

The Sahibayn (the two) hold that the four-fifths go to the mukhatt lahu (the original allocatee of the land from the Imam at conquest) or his heirs, because his constructive possession is specific (khususi) and thus effective over hidden property. The passage then answers an objection: constructive possession does not confer ownership in general, but if it is specific (like a single owner), it does. The example of the fish and pearl illustrates this: the owner of the fish becomes owner of the pearl found inside, even if he didn't physically possess it initially.

Finally, the ruling on sale of land: if the mukhatt lahu sells the land and later a treasure is discovered, the treasure still belongs to the seller (mukhatt lahu) because it is considered a deposit (wadi'ah) within the land; it does not pass to the buyer, unlike minerals which are part of the land and thus transfer with it. This is another furu' (branch) deriving from the principle of specific constructive possession.

Overall, the passage demonstrates the Hanafi use of rational proofs (istihsan) and the principle of thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequences) in fiqh.

Key Terms
مختط لہ (mukhatt lahu): The person to whom the Imam initially allocated a plot of conquered land by drawing a line (khatt) to define its boundaries; the original grante.
احراز (ihraz): The act of taking possession and securing property; here it refers to the finder's act of extracting and safeguarding the treasure.
قبضہ حکمی (qabdah hukmi): Constructive possession; possession in the eyes of the law without physical control, e.g., the general possession of ghazis over conquered lands.

Key Takeaway
The ownership of pre-Islamic treasure in conquered lands hinges on whether the prior constructive possession of the original allocatee is specific enough to override the finder's actual possession.

Ambiguous Treasure (Mush Tabih): Default Rule and Later Adaptation
Ruling on ambiguous treasure (mush tabih) with intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default ruling for ambiguous treasure and explain the later opinion based on changing circumstances.

Core Idea
Default: ambiguous treasure is pre-Islamic (khums wajib); later view: treat as Islamic due to antiquity.

In this passage, the author addresses a treasure that lacks any identifying signs. The Hanafi school has a default rule, but also acknowledges a practical adaptation due to the passage of centuries. This lesson clarifies both positions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Treasures can have Islamic signs, pre-Islamic signs, or be ambiguous (mush tabih). Focus on the ambiguous case.
The text says 'neither signs of Islam nor signs of disbelief'.
2
Understand the default ruling
The zahir madhhab treats ambiguous treasure as pre-Islamic (kufri) because the original basis (asl) is kufr. This requires khums (1/5) when found.
The reasoning: before the spread of Islam, most treasures were pre-Islamic.
3
Examine the later opinion
A later opinion (qīla) holds that after centuries of Islamic rule, such treasure is likely Islamic. Thus no khums, it is treated as luqtah (lost property) or owned by the finder.
The rationale: it is improbable that pre-Islamic treasure remained undiscovered for so long.
4
Note the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether khums is wajib. The later view is often adopted in practice due to changed circumstances.
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, but later Hanafi practice leans toward Islamic treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور اگر دفینہ مشتبہ اس پر نہ علامات اسلام ہیں اور نہ علامات کفر ہیں تو ظاہر مذہب یہ ہے کہ اس کو جاہلی اور کفری قرار دیا جائے گا کیونکہ اس کا کفری ہونا یہی اصل ہے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ ہمارے زمانے میں اس کو اسلامی قرار دیا جائے گا کیونکہ عہد اسلام بہت قدیم ہو چکا ہے اس لئے ظاہر یہی ہے کہ یہ کفار کے ہاتھوں کا دفن کیا ہوا نہیں ہے بلکہ اہل اسلام کا دفن کردہ ہے۔

کوئی شخص دار الحرب میں امان کے ساتھ داخل ہوا اور اس نے رکاز (دفینہ) پایا تو اس کا مالک کون ہے وَمَنْ دَخَلَ دَارَ الحَرْبِ بِأَمَانٍ فَوَجَدَ فِي دَارِ بَعْضِهِمْ رِكَازا رَدَهُ عَلَيْهِمْ تَحَرُّزًا عَنِ الْغَدْرِ لِأَنَّ مَا فِي الدَّارِ فِي يَدِ أَهْلِهَا خُصُوصًا وَإِنْ وَجَدَهُ فِي الصَّحْرَاءِ فَهُوَ لَهُ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِى يَدِ أَحَدٍ عَلَى الْخُصُوصِ فَلَا يُعَدَّ غَدْرًا وَلَا شَيْ فِيهِ لأَنَّهُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمُتَلَصِّص غَيْرُ مُجَاهِرٍ
ترجمہ: اور جو شخص امان لے کر دار الحرب میں داخل ہوا۔ پھر کسی حربی کے مکان میں رکاز پایا تو اس رکاز کو مکان کے مالکوں کی طرف واپس کر دے۔ عذر اور بدعہدی سے بچنے کے لئے۔ کیونکہ گھر میں جو چیز ہے۔ وہ خصوصاً مالک مکان کے قبضہ میں ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے صحرا میں پایا تو وہ اس کا ہے کیونکہ وہ کسی کے خاص قبضہ میں نہیں۔ اس میں غدر (بدعہدی) نہیں۔ اور اس پر کچھ واجب نہیں کیونکہ وہ چور کی مانند ہے جو ظاہر نہ ہو۔
And if the treasure is ambiguous (mush tabih) – having neither signs of Islam nor signs of disbelief – then the apparent (zāhir) madhhab is that it will be considered pre-Islamic and disbelieving, because its being disbelieving is the original basis (asl). And it has been said that in our time it will be considered Islamic, because the era of Islam has become very ancient, therefore it is apparent that this is not buried by the hands of disbelievers, but rather is buried by the people of Islam.

A person who enters Dar al-Harb with aman (safe conduct) and finds rikaz (treasure): what is its ownership? "And whoever enters Dar al-Harb with aman and finds a rikaz in the house of some of them, he returns it to them, to avoid treachery (ghadr). Because what is in the house is specifically in the possession of its owner. And if he finds it in the desert (sahra), then it belongs to him, because it is not in the specific possession of anyone. This is not considered treachery, and there is nothing (obligatory) on him, because he is like a stealthy thief (mutalassis ghair mujāhir)." Translation: And whoever enters Dar al-Harb with aman. Then he finds rikaz in the house of a harbi, he returns that rikaz to the owners of the house, to avoid treachery. Because what is in the house is specifically in the possession of its owner. And if he finds it in the desert, then it is his, because it is not in the specific possession of anyone. There is no treachery in it, and nothing is obligatory on him, because he is like a stealthy thief who does not appear openly.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage covers two remaining issues: ambiguous treasure and treasure in enemy territory.

First, for a treasure that lacks both Islamic and pre-Islamic signs (mush tabih), the default (zāhir madhhab) is to treat it as pre-Islamic, thus requiring khums. The reasoning is that the original state (asl) of such treasures before Islam was that they belonged to the pre-Islamic era. However, a later opinion (possibly from later Hanafi scholars) says that in our time (i.e., after many centuries of Muslim rule), such treasure is likely to be Islamic, because it is improbable that pre-Islamic treasure would remain undiscovered for so long. Therefore, it should be treated as Islamic (i.e., luqtah or considered as belonging to Muslims). This is a practical adaptation based on changing circumstances.

Second, the text addresses a person who enters Dar al-Harb (enemy territory) with a safe conduct (aman). If he finds treasure in a house (or private property) of a harbi (enemy non-Muslim), he must return it to the owner to avoid ghadr (treachery). Trust is given to the harbi through the aman, and taking hidden property from his private dwelling would breach that trust. The treasure is considered in the specific possession of the homeowner.

However, if the finder discovers the treasure in the desert (sahra) or open land with no specific owner, he can keep it. There is no treachery because it is not in anyone's specific possession. The text likens the finder to a 'stealthy thief' (mutalassis ghair mujāhir) – this is not an insult but a legal analogy: just as a thief who takes something from an unowned place is not punished in the same way, similarly, finding treasure in the open does not breach the aman. No khums is mentioned here; the rule is that the finder becomes the owner.

The passage concludes with a short Arabic quotation and its Urdu translation. The overall lesson shows the Hanafi emphasis on consistency in legal principle (asl) and practical adaptation (in the case of ambiguous treasure) as well as the importance of ethical conduct (aman and avoiding ghadr) in inter-community relations.

Key Terms
دفینہ مشتبہ (mush tabih): Treasure with no clear signs of being Islamic or pre-Islamic.
ظاہر مذہب (zahir madhhab): The apparent or preferred position of the school, as distinct from later opinions.
اصل (asl): Original state or presumption; here, the default of pre-Islamic status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir madhhab (apparent school), what is the ruling on ambiguous treasure (mush tabih)?
A) It is luqtah, no khums.
B) Khums (1/5) is wajib.
C) It belongs to the state.
D) It must be spent in charity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school balances default rules with practical adaptation, as seen in the evolving ruling on ambiguous treasure.

Finding Treasure in Dar al-Harb with Aman: House vs Desert
Ruling on finding treasure in Dar al-Harb under aman, focusing on possession and trust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling for treasure found in Dar al-Harb under aman, distinguishing between house and desert.

Core Idea
Treasure in a private house must be returned to owner; in desert it belongs to finder, to avoid treachery.

This passage deals with a Muslim who enters enemy territory with safe conduct (aman) and finds buried treasure (rikaz). The ruling hinges on the concept of possession (yad) and the prohibition of treachery (ghadr). Understanding this distinction is crucial for maintaining trust in inter-community relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A Muslim enters Dar al-Harb (enemy territory) with aman (safe conduct). He finds rikaz (buried treasure).
The aman creates a contract of protection and trust.
2
Distinguish two locations
If the treasure is found in a house (dar) of a harbi (non-Muslim enemy), it is considered in the specific possession (yad khass) of the owner. If found in the desert (sahra) or open land, it has no specific owner.
Possession (yad) is key to determining treachery.
3
Apply ruling for house
Return the treasure to the owner of the house. Keeping it would be ghadr (treachery), a major sin, because the aman contract requires trust.
The text uses 'li-ajli al-ghadr' (to avoid treachery).
4
Apply ruling for desert
The finder may keep the treasure. There is no treachery because it is not in anyone's specific possession. No khums is mentioned; the finder becomes owner.
The analogy is made to a stealthy thief (mutalassis) who takes without confrontation – here it is not punishable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور اگر دفینہ مشتبہ اس پر نہ علامات اسلام ہیں اور نہ علامات کفر ہیں تو ظاہر مذہب یہ ہے کہ اس کو جاہلی اور کفری قرار دیا جائے گا کیونکہ اس کا کفری ہونا یہی اصل ہے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ ہمارے زمانے میں اس کو اسلامی قرار دیا جائے گا کیونکہ عہد اسلام بہت قدیم ہو چکا ہے اس لئے ظاہر یہی ہے کہ یہ کفار کے ہاتھوں کا دفن کیا ہوا نہیں ہے بلکہ اہل اسلام کا دفن کردہ ہے۔

کوئی شخص دار الحرب میں امان کے ساتھ داخل ہوا اور اس نے رکاز (دفینہ) پایا تو اس کا مالک کون ہے وَمَنْ دَخَلَ دَارَ الحَرْبِ بِأَمَانٍ فَوَجَدَ فِي دَارِ بَعْضِهِمْ رِكَازا رَدَهُ عَلَيْهِمْ تَحَرُّزًا عَنِ الْغَدْرِ لِأَنَّ مَا فِي الدَّارِ فِي يَدِ أَهْلِهَا خُصُوصًا وَإِنْ وَجَدَهُ فِي الصَّحْرَاءِ فَهُوَ لَهُ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِى يَدِ أَحَدٍ عَلَى الْخُصُوصِ فَلَا يُعَدَّ غَدْرًا وَلَا شَيْ فِيهِ لأَنَّهُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمُتَلَصِّص غَيْرُ مُجَاهِرٍ
ترجمہ: اور جو شخص امان لے کر دار الحرب میں داخل ہوا۔ پھر کسی حربی کے مکان میں رکاز پایا تو اس رکاز کو مکان کے مالکوں کی طرف واپس کر دے۔ عذر اور بدعہدی سے بچنے کے لئے۔ کیونکہ گھر میں جو چیز ہے۔ وہ خصوصاً مالک مکان کے قبضہ میں ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے صحرا میں پایا تو وہ اس کا ہے کیونکہ وہ کسی کے خاص قبضہ میں نہیں۔ اس میں غدر (بدعہدی) نہیں۔ اور اس پر کچھ واجب نہیں کیونکہ وہ چور کی مانند ہے جو ظاہر نہ ہو۔
And if the treasure is ambiguous (mush tabih) – having neither signs of Islam nor signs of disbelief – then the apparent (zāhir) madhhab is that it will be considered pre-Islamic and disbelieving, because its being disbelieving is the original basis (asl). And it has been said that in our time it will be considered Islamic, because the era of Islam has become very ancient, therefore it is apparent that this is not buried by the hands of disbelievers, but rather is buried by the people of Islam.

A person who enters Dar al-Harb with aman (safe conduct) and finds rikaz (treasure): what is its ownership? "And whoever enters Dar al-Harb with aman and finds a rikaz in the house of some of them, he returns it to them, to avoid treachery (ghadr). Because what is in the house is specifically in the possession of its owner. And if he finds it in the desert (sahra), then it belongs to him, because it is not in the specific possession of anyone. This is not considered treachery, and there is nothing (obligatory) on him, because he is like a stealthy thief (mutalassis ghair mujāhir)." Translation: And whoever enters Dar al-Harb with aman. Then he finds rikaz in the house of a harbi, he returns that rikaz to the owners of the house, to avoid treachery. Because what is in the house is specifically in the possession of its owner. And if he finds it in the desert, then it is his, because it is not in the specific possession of anyone. There is no treachery in it, and nothing is obligatory on him, because he is like a stealthy thief who does not appear openly.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage covers two remaining issues: ambiguous treasure and treasure in enemy territory.

First, for a treasure that lacks both Islamic and pre-Islamic signs (mush tabih), the default (zāhir madhhab) is to treat it as pre-Islamic, thus requiring khums. The reasoning is that the original state (asl) of such treasures before Islam was that they belonged to the pre-Islamic era. However, a later opinion (possibly from later Hanafi scholars) says that in our time (i.e., after many centuries of Muslim rule), such treasure is likely to be Islamic, because it is improbable that pre-Islamic treasure would remain undiscovered for so long. Therefore, it should be treated as Islamic (i.e., luqtah or considered as belonging to Muslims). This is a practical adaptation based on changing circumstances.

Second, the text addresses a person who enters Dar al-Harb (enemy territory) with a safe conduct (aman). If he finds treasure in a house (or private property) of a harbi (enemy non-Muslim), he must return it to the owner to avoid ghadr (treachery). Trust is given to the harbi through the aman, and taking hidden property from his private dwelling would breach that trust. The treasure is considered in the specific possession of the homeowner.

However, if the finder discovers the treasure in the desert (sahra) or open land with no specific owner, he can keep it. There is no treachery because it is not in anyone's specific possession. The text likens the finder to a 'stealthy thief' (mutalassis ghair mujāhir) – this is not an insult but a legal analogy: just as a thief who takes something from an unowned place is not punished in the same way, similarly, finding treasure in the open does not breach the aman. No khums is mentioned here; the rule is that the finder becomes the owner.

The passage concludes with a short Arabic quotation and its Urdu translation. The overall lesson shows the Hanafi emphasis on consistency in legal principle (asl) and practical adaptation (in the case of ambiguous treasure) as well as the importance of ethical conduct (aman and avoiding ghadr) in inter-community relations.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (Dar al-Harb): Territory under the rule of non-Muslims with whom Muslims are in a state of war.
امان (aman): A safe conduct granted to enable a person (Muslim or non-Muslim) to enter enemy territory without being harmed.
غدر (ghadr): Breach of trust or treachery, particularly violating an aman agreement.
متلصص (mutalassis): A stealthy thief who takes without confrontation; used analogically to describe the finder in the desert.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person with aman finds treasure in the house of a harbi, he must return it to avoid gh? What does 'gh' refer to?
A) Ghanimah (booty)
B) Ghadr (treachery)
C) Ghurr (deception)
D) Ghasab (usurpation)

Key Takeaway
The aman contract creates a duty of trust; taking treasure from private property breaches it, but desert treasure does not.

Treasure in Granted Land: Ownership and Sale
Ruling on ownership of treasure in granted land and its sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a treasure found in land granted by the Imam belongs to the grantee even after sale, and apply the fish-pearl analogy to similar cases.

Core Idea
Ownership of a container gives ownership of its hidden contents—treasure in land is like a pearl in a fish.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific case: treasure found in land that the Imam granted to a person (mukhattat lahu). The ruling is that the treasure belongs to the grantee or his heirs, even if the land is later sold. The Hanafi school uses an analogy with a fisherman who finds a pearl in a fish he caught. However, an objection arises because the grantee's possession is only constructive, not physical. The commentator resolves this by distinguishing between general constructive possession (as with the army) and specific constructive possession (as with an individual grantee).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the legal case? The act is finding treasure (rikaz) in land granted by the Imam. The ruling is that the treasure belongs to the grantee (mukhattat lahu) or his heirs.
The ruling applies even after the land is sold.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is an analogy (qiyas) with a fisherman who catches a fish and finds a pearl inside. The pearl belongs to the fisherman because he owns the fish.
This is a clear analogy where the land is like the fish and the treasure is like the pearl.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is ownership of the container (fish/land) giving ownership of the hidden content through specific constructive possession. The Hanafi school holds that specific constructive possession (qabd hukmi khas) is sufficient for ownership, unlike general constructive possession (e.g., the army's possession of conquered land).
The distinction between specific and general possession is key to answering the objection.
4
Note the objection and response
An opponent argues that the grantee's possession is only constructive (hukmi), not actual, so it should not confer ownership. The Hanafi response: constructive possession does confer ownership when it is specific to one individual, not general like the army's.
This shows how the Hanafi school refines the concept of possession.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the treasure belongs to the mukhattat lahu. This is consistent with the Hanafi emphasis on land ownership over finder's rights.
Shafi'is disagree, holding that rikaz requires khums and goes to the finder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكوة

... مختط لہ وہ شخص ہے جس کو امام نے ابتدائے فتح میں زمین کے اس قطعہ کا مالک کر دیا ہے اور اس کے حدود پر خط کھینچ کر اس کے طول و عرض کو متعین کر دیا ہے پس چارٹس کا حقدار ہی محفط لہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر یہ نہ ہو تو اس کے وارث حقدار ہوں گئے اگر چہ کئی پشت نیچے ہوں کیونکہ فتح کے بعد سب سے پہلا قبضہ اس مختط لہ کا ہے طرفین کی دلیل پر ایک اشکال ہے وہ یہ کہ دفینہ پر خط لہ کا قبضہ اگر چہ سابق ہے لیکن یہ قبضہ حکما ہے کیونکہ حقیقہ قبضہ واجد (پانے والے) کا ہے اور حکمی قبضہ سے دفینہ کا کوئی مالک نہیں ہوتا جیسا کہ غازیوں کے بارے میں گذر چکا ہے کہ وہ اس کے مالک نہیں ہوتے ہیں اس لئے غازیوں کا قبضہ بھی حکما ہے اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حکمی قبضہ سے بلا شبہ ملک ثابت نہیں ہوتی مگر یہ اس وقت ہے جبکہ حکمی قبضہ علی سبیل العموم ہو جیسا کہ غازیوں کا قبضہ علی سبیل العموم ہوتا ہے کہ عمومی طور پر تمام غازی اس کے مالک ہیں لیکن اگر حکمی قبضہ خصوصی طور پر ہو یعنی متعین طور پر ایک ہی آدمی مالک ہو تو اس قبضہ حکمی کی وجہ سے اندرون زمین کی چیز و نکا مالک بھی ہو جائے گا اگر چہ اس کا حقیقی قبضہ ظاہر ارض پر ہے مثلا ایک آدمی نے مچھلی کا شکار کیا اور اس کے پر میں موتی نکلا تو اس موتی کا مالک بھی یہی شخص ہو گا جو مچھلی کا مالک ہے۔ ثُمَّ
ثم بالبيع الخ سے یہ فرمایا کہ خط لہ نے اگر زمین فروخت کردنی پھر اس زمین میں سے کوئی دفینہ بر آمد ہوا تو بھی چارٹس مخط لہ کے ہوں گے کیونکہ دفینہ زمین کے اندر ودیعت رکھا ہوا ہے اس لئے زمین فروخت کرنے سے وہ دفینہ اس کے مالک سے خارج نہ ہوگا جیسے کسی نے شکار کرنے کے بعد مچھلی فروخت کی اور مچھلی کے پیٹ سے موتی نکلا تو یہ موتی فروخت کر دینے سے اس کی ملک سے خارج نہیں ہوگا بر خلاف معدن کے کہ وہ زمین کی بیع سے مشتری کی طرف منتقل ہو جائے گی کیونکہ معدن (كان) زمین کے اجزاء میں سے ہے پس جب زمین مشتری کی طرف منتقل ہو گی تو اس کے تمام اجزاء بھی مشتری کی طرف منتقل ہو جائیں گے۔
Book of Zakat. The mukhattat lahu is the person whom the Imam, at the beginning of the conquest, made the owner of that piece of land and drew lines on its boundaries to define its length and width. So [the one entitled] to the quarter? is the mukhattat lahu. If he is not present, his heirs are entitled, even if several generations down, because the first possession after the conquest belongs to the mukhattat lahu. There is an objection to both proofs: that the possession of the mukhattat lahu over the treasure, even if prior, is a constructive possession, because actual possession belongs to the finder, and constructive possession does not make one owner of the treasure, as has passed regarding the ghazis that they are not owners. So also the ghazis' possession is constructive. The answer: Constructive possession does not establish ownership without doubt, but that is when constructive possession is general, as the ghazis' possession is general. But if constructive possession is specific, i.e., one specific person is the owner, then due to that constructive possession he also becomes the owner of what is inside the land, even if his actual possession is on the surface. For example, a man catches a fish and a pearl is found in its belly; that pearl belongs to that same person who owns the fish. Then: from 'thumma bi al-bay'i' etc., it says that if the mukhattat lahu sold the land and then a treasure is found in it, still the quarter? belongs to the mukhattat lahu, because the treasure is deposited inside the land; selling the land does not remove the treasure from his ownership. Like if someone sold a fish after catching it and a pearl is found in its belly, that pearl is not removed from his ownership by the sale, unlike a mine, which transfers to the buyer with the land because the mine is part of the land's components.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on treasure (rikaz) found in land granted by the Imam to a person (mukhattat lahu). The commentator expands the base text of Al-Hidayah. The ruling states that the treasure belongs to the mukhattat lahu or his heirs in perpetuity, even after sale of the land. The proof is the analogy of a fish and its pearl: just as the pearl belongs to the fisherman who caught the fish, the treasure belongs to the owner of the land because his possession, though constructive, is specific and not general like that of the army. The objection that constructive possession does not give ownership is answered: general constructive possession (as of ghazis) does not, but specific constructive possession does. The treasure is considered a deposit in the land, so sale does not transfer it, unlike a mine which is part of the land's essence. This follows the Hanafi principle that hidden wealth follows the owner of the land in a specific grant.

Key Terms
مُخَطَّطٌ لَهُ (mukhattat lahu): A person whom the Imam grants a specific piece of conquered land by marking its boundaries.
قبض حکمی (qabd hukmi): Constructive possession without physical control, e.g., ownership by legal assignment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer of the land
B) The mukhattat lahu (seller)
C) The state treasury (bait al-mal)
D) The finder of the treasure

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, specific constructive possession of land confers ownership of hidden treasure, even after sale.

Treasure Found by a Musta'min in Dar al-Harb
Ruling on treasure found by a musta'min in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rikaz found by a musta'min in Dar al-Harb, distinguish between house and desert, and explain the proof from the hadith on fulfilling covenants.

Core Idea
A musta'min must return treasure found in a house to avoid treachery; treasure in an ownerless desert is his, with no khums.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from al-Hidayah regarding a Muslim who enters enemy territory with safe conduct (aman) and discovers rikaz (treasure). The ruling hinges on the location of the find: inside a specific person's house versus open, ownerless desert. We will explore the legal reasoning rooted in the prohibition of treachery (ghadr) and the nature of possession, along with the hadith proof cited in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
A person (musta'min) enters Dar al-Harb with aman. Two sub-scenarios: finds treasure in a house vs in the desert.
2
State the rulings
House: return treasure to owner. Desert: treasure is for finder, no khums.
3
Locate the proof
The hadith 'فِي الْعُهُودِ وَفَاء لَا غَدْرَ' (In covenants there is fulfillment, no treachery) is cited for the house case.
Thubut: zanni, Dalala: zanni
4
Trace the reasoning
For the house: the treasure is in specific possession of the house owner, so taking it would breach the aman covenant. For the desert: no specific possession, so no treachery. Also, khums is not due because it is not booty taken by force.
The finder is likened to a secret thief, not a conqueror.
5
Identify the underlying principle
The principle is avoiding ghadr (treachery) in covenants. The aman creates a trust relationship that must be honored.
This reflects Hanafi emphasis on fulfilling covenants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ دَخَلَ دَارَ الْحَرْبِ بِأَمَانٍ فَوَجَدَ فِي دَارِ بَعْضِهِمْ رِكَازًا رَدَّهُ عَلَيْهِمْ تَحَرُّزًا عَنِ الْغَدْرِ لِأَنَّ مَا فِي الدَّارِ فِي يَدِ صَاحِبِهَا خُصُوصًا وَإِنْ وَجَدَهُ فِى الصَّحْرَاءِ فَهُوَ لَهُ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي يَدِ أَحَدٍ عَلَى الْخُصُوصِ فَلَا يُعَدَّ غَدْرًا وَلَا شَيْءَ فِيهِ لِأَنَّهُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمُتَلَصِّصِ غَيْرِ مُجَاهِرٍ

ترجمہ اور جوشخص امان لے کر دار الحرب میں داخل ہوا۔ پھر کسی حربی کے مکان میں رکاز پایا تو اس رکاز کو مکان کے مالکوں کی طرف واپس کر دے۔ عذر اور بدعہدی سے بچنے کے لئے۔ کیونکہ گھر میں جو چیز ہے۔ وہ خصوصا مالک مکان کے قبضہ میں ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے رکاز دارالحرب کے صحرا میں پائی تو یہ رکاز اسی پانے والے کی ہے کیونکہ یہ کسی کے خصوصی قبضہ میں نہیں ہے تو یہ غدر شمار نہ ہوگا۔ اور اس میں کوئی چیز واجب نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ وہ خص خفیہ طور پر مال چرانے والے کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔

تشریح اگر کوئی شخص امان لے کر دار الحرب میں داخل ہوا پھر اس نے وہاں کسی حربی کے گھر میں رکاز پایا رکاز خواہ معدن ہو یا دفینہ ہو تو اس رکاز کو مالک مکان کو واپس کر دے تاکہ غدر اور بد عہدی کرنا لازم نہ آئے ۔ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا کہ "فِي الْعُهُودِ وَفَاء لَا غَدْرَ" یعنی عہد و پیمان میں ایفاء ہونا چاہئے غدر اور بدعبدی نہ کرے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ جو کچھ گھر میں ہے وہ خاص طور پر مالک مکان کے قبضہ میں ہے یعنی اس پر مالک مکان کا قبضہ ہے اگر چہ کی قبضہ ہے لہذا اس رکاز کو لے لینا بد عہدی ہے۔ اور اگر اس کو سرزمین دار الحرب میں ایسے جنگل میں پایا جس کا کوئی مالک نہیں ہے تو یہ رکاز اسی پانے والے کا ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ رکاز کسی کے خصوصی قبضہ میں نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اس کو لینا شرعا غدر شمار نہ ہو گا ۔ اور اس رکاز میں خمس بھی واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ شمس اس مال میں واجب ہوتا ہے جو غنیمت کے معنی میں ہو۔ اور مال غنیمت وہ کہلاتا ہے جو اہل حرب کے قبضہ میں تھا پھر مسلمان حملہ آور ہو کر اس پر غلبہ پاگئے ہوں ۔ لیکن یہ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ خص خفیہ طور پر مال چرانے والے کے مانند ہے۔ پس جب یہ مال غنیمت کے معنی میں نہیں ہے تو اس میں شمس بھی واجب نہ ہوگا۔
And whoever enters Dar al-Harb with aman (safe conduct) and finds rikaz (treasure) in someone's house, he should return it to them, to avoid treachery, because what is in the house is specifically in the possession of its owner. If he finds it in the desert, it is his, because it is not in anyone's specific possession, so it is not considered treachery, and there is no khums on it because he is in the position of a non-conspicuous thief.

Translation: And a person who enters Dar al-Harb with aman, then finds rikaz in a harbi's house, he should return that rikaz to the house owners, to avoid treachery and breach of covenant. Because what is in the house is specifically in the owner's possession. And if he finds rikaz in the desert of Dar al-Harb, this rikaz is for the finder himself, because it is not in anyone's specific possession, so it is not considered treachery. And no khums is obligatory therein, because he is in the position of a secret thief.

Explanation: If a person enters Dar al-Harb with aman and then finds rikaz (whether mine or treasure) in a harbi's house, he should return it to avoid treachery and breach of covenant. The Prophet ﷺ said: 'In covenants there is fulfillment, no treachery.' The proof: whatever is in the house is specifically in the owner's possession, so taking it would be breach of trust. If he finds it in an ownerless desert land, the treasure is his because it is not in anyone's specific possession, so taking it is not legally treacherous. And no khums is obligatory because khums is only due on booty, and this is not booty as it was not taken by force from the enemy.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from hadith on treasure found by a musta'min (a person under safe conduct) in Dar al-Harb. The ruling distinguishes between treasure found inside a specific person's house and treasure found in an ownerless desert. In the first case, the treasure must be returned to the house owner to avoid treachery (ghadr). In the second case, the finder owns it and no khums is due. The proof is the hadith 'In covenants there is fulfillment, no treachery.' The rationale is that possession of the house extends to hidden treasure, so taking it violates the aman. In the desert, no specific owner exists, so taking it is not treacherous. Khums is not due because the treasure is not considered ghanimah (booty) as it was not taken by force from the enemy; rather, it is likened to a secret theft which does not entail khums. This ruling reflects the Hanafi emphasis on preserving covenants (aman) and the specific legal concept of 'ghadr'.

Key Terms
غدر (ghadr): Treachery, breach of covenant; specifically violating the trust of aman.
مستأمن (musta'min): A person from dar al-harb who enters Islamic territory with aman, or a Muslim who enters dar al-harb with aman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim enters Dar al-Harb with aman and finds a treasure in a Harbi's house. What must he do according to the Hanafi school?
A) Keep it as booty and pay khums
B) Return it to the house owner to avoid treachery
C) Give it to the Muslim ruler
D) Leave it and not touch it

Key Takeaway
Treasure found by a musta'min in a house must be returned to avoid treachery; treasure in an ownerless desert belongs to the finder without khums.
